# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repositories (version-pinned mirrors):**

- `github.com/sg-evals/istio--f8af3cae` — use `repo:^github.com/sg-evals/istio--f8af3cae$` filter

Scope ALL keyword_search/nls_search queries to these repos.
Use the repo name as the `repo` parameter for read_file/go_to_definition/find_references.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Generate Developer Onboarding Guide for Istio Control Plane

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/istio--f8af3cae (mirror of istio/istio)
**Output:** Write your guide to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce a developer onboarding guide for Istio's control plane. This guide should help a new contributor understand how to build Istio, navigate the codebase, and make their first contribution.

## Scope

Your guide must cover all of the following:

### 1. Build Prerequisites
- Required Go version, tools, and environment variables
- How to clone the repository with all submodules
- How to build the core control plane binaries (`pilot-discovery`, `istiod`)
- How to run the test suite

### 2. Architecture Overview
- Istio's control plane components and their responsibilities (Pilot, Citadel, Galley — now merged into istiod)
- The xDS protocol and how istiod pushes configuration to Envoy proxies
- Key packages: `pilot/pkg/`, `security/pkg/`, `galley/pkg/`
- How the service registry integrates with Kubernetes

### 3. First Contribution Workflow
- How to find good first issues
- How to run linters and pre-commit checks
- How to write and run unit tests for a change
- How to submit a PR (required reviewers, CI gates)

## Quality Bar

- Reference specific Makefile targets, Go packages, or scripts
- Architecture section must explain at least one data flow end-to-end (e.g., service discovery to xDS push)
- Do not fabricate commands — verify against actual Makefile and scripts in the repo

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not simply reproduce the README
- Do not include Kubernetes operator/installation instructions (focus on developer workflow only)

{"content":"1: # Architecture of Istiod\n2: \n3: This document describes the high level architecture of the Istio control plane, Istiod.\n4: Istiod is structured as a modular monolith, housing a wide range of functionality from certificate signing, proxy configuration (XDS), traditional Kubernetes controllers, and more.\n5: \n6: ## Proxy Configuration\n7: \n8: Istiod's primary role - and most code - is to dynamically configure proxies (Envoy sidecars and ingress, gRPC, ztunnel, and more). This roughly consists of 3 parts:\n9: 1. Config ingestion (inputs to the system)\n10: 1. Config translation\n11: 1. Config serving (XDS)\n12: \n13: ### Config Ingestion\n14: \n15: Istio reads from over 20 different resources types, and aggregates them together to build the proxy configuration. These resources can be sourced from Kubernetes (via watches), files, or over xDS; Kubernetes is by far the most common usage, though.\n16: \n17: Primarily for historical reasons, ingestion is split into a few components.\n18: \n19: #### ConfigStore\n20: \n21: The `ConfigStore` reads a variety of resources and exposes them over a standard interface (Get, List, etc). These types are wrapped in a common `config.Config` struct, contrasting with typical Kubernetes clients which use per-resource types. The most common is reading from Kubernetes via the `crdclient` package.\n22: \n23: ```mermaid\n24: graph TD\n25:     subgraph ConfigStore\n26:         xcs(XDS Client)\n27:         ccs(CRD Client)\n28:         fcs(Filesystem Client)\n29:         acs(Aggregate)\n30:         xcs-->acs\n31:         ccs-->acs\n32:         fcs-->acs\n33:     end\n34: ```\n35: \n36: #### ServiceDiscovery\n37: \n38: The other primary interface is the ServiceDiscovery. Similar to ConfigStore, this aggregates over a variety of res

Perfect! I've created a comprehensive developer onboarding guide for Istio's control plane. Here's what I've included:

## Guide Contents

### 1. **Build Prerequisites** (Verified from repo)
- Go 1.25.0 requirement
- Two build modes (native and container-based using `BUILD_WITH_CONTAINER`)
- Cloning with submodules
- Specific Makefile targets:
  - `make build` - all binaries
  - `make pilot-discovery` - main control plane
  - `make istioctl` - CLI tool
  - `make pilot-agent` - data plane agent
- Unit and integration test commands
- Actual binary paths and verification steps

### 2. **Architecture Overview** (From architecture documentation)
- Control plane components (Pilot merged with Citadel/Galley into istiod)
- Directory organization with specific package roles
- **xDS protocol explanation** with all resource types (LDS, RDS, CDS, EDS)
- **End-to-end data flow**: Service discovery → Config ingestion → PushContext → Config generation → XDS Server → Envoy
- Key packages with code locations:
  - `pilot/pkg/xds/` - XDS server implementation
  - `pilot/pkg/networking/core/` - Envoy config builders (clusters, listeners, routes)
  - `pilot/pkg/model/` - Core data structures (Proxy, PushContext, Service)
  - `pilot/pkg/serviceregistry/` - Service discovery from K8s
  - `security/pkg/` - mTLS and authorization policies

### 3. **First Contribution Workflow**
- How to find good-first-issues on GitHub
- Complete development setup (fork, clone, feature branches)
- Code change examples in actual Istio patterns
- Running linters: `make format` and `make lint`
- Writing unit tests with Go testing patterns
- Full PR submission process
- Release notes requirements
- Debugging and troubleshooting
- Complete testing checklist before pushing

Istio\u2019s powerful features provide a uniform and more efficient way to secure, connect, and monitor services. Istio is the path to load balancing, service-to-service authentication, and monitoring \u2013 with few or no service code changes.\\n18: \\n19: - For in-depth information about how to use Istio, visit [istio.io](https://istio.io)\\n20: - To ask questions and get assistance from our community, visit [GitHub Discussions](https://github.com/istio/istio/discussions)\\n21: - To learn how to participate in our overall community, visit [our community page](https://istio.io/about/community)\\n22: \\n23: In this README:\\n24: \\n25: - [Introduction](#introduction)\\n26: - [Repositories](#repositories)\\n27: - [Issue management](#issue-management)\\n28: \\n29: In addition, here are some other documents you may wish to read:\\n30: \\n31: - [Istio Community](https://github.com/istio/community#istio-community) - describes how to get involved and contribute to the Istio project\\n32: - [Istio Developer's Guide](https://github.com/istio/istio/wiki/Preparing-for-Development) - explains how to set up and use an Istio development environment\\n33: - [Project Conventions](https://github.com/istio/istio/wiki/Development-Conventions) - describes the conventions we use within the code base\\n34: - [Creating Fast and Lean Code](https://github.com/istio/istio/wiki/Writing-Fast-and-Lean-Code) - performance-oriented advice and guidelines for the code base\\n35: \\n36: You'll find many other useful documents on our [Wiki](https://github.com/istio/istio/wiki).\\n37: \\n38: ## Introduction\\n39: \\n40: [Istio](https://istio.io/latest/docs/concepts/what-is-istio/) is an open platform for providing a uniform way to [integrate\\n41: microservices](https://istio.io/latest/docs/examples/microservices-istio/), manage [traffic flow](https://istio.io/latest/docs/concepts/traffic-management/) across microservices, enforce policies\\n42: and aggregate telemetry data. Istio's control plane provides an abstraction\\n43: layer over the underlying cluster management platform, such as Kubernetes.\\n44: \\n45: Istio is composed of these components:\\n46: \\n47: - **Envoy** - Sidecar proxies per microservice to handle ingress/egress traffic\\n48:    between services in the cluster and from a service to external\\n49:    services. The proxies form a _secure microservice mesh_ providing a rich\\n50:    set of functions like discovery, rich layer-7 routing, circuit breakers,\\n51:    policy enforcement and telemetry recording/reporting\\n52:    functions.\\n53: \\n54:   > Note: The service mesh is not an overlay network. It\\n55:   > simplifies and enhances how microservices in an application talk to each\\n56:   > other over the network provided by the underlying platform.\\n57: \\n58: * **Ztunnel** - A lightweight data plane proxy written in Rust,\\n59:     used in Ambient mesh mode to provide secure connectivity and observability for workloads without sidecar proxies.\\n60: \\n61: - **Istiod** - The Istio control plane. It provides service discovery, configuration and certificate management.\\n62: \\n63: ## Repositories\\n64: \\n65: The Istio project is divided across a few GitHub repositories:\\n66: \\n67: - [istio/api](https://github.com/istio/api). This repository defines\\n68: component-level APIs and common configuration formats for the Istio platform.\\n69: \\n70: - [istio/community](https://github.com/istio/community). This repository contains\\n71: information on the Istio community, including the various documents that govern\\n72: the Istio open source project.\\n73: \\n74: - [istio/istio](README.md). This is the main code repository. It hosts Istio's\\n75: core components, install artifacts, and sample programs. It includes:\\n76: \\n77:     - [istioctl](istioctl/). This directory contains code for the\\n78: [_istioctl_](https://istio.io/latest/docs/reference/commands/istioctl/) command line utility.\\n79: \\n80:     - [pilot](pilot/). This directory\\n81: contains platform-specific code to populate the\\n82: [abstract service model](https://istio.io/docs/concepts/traffic-management/#pilot), dynamically reconfigure the proxies\\n83: when the application topology changes, as well as translate\\n84: [routing rules](https://istio.io/latest/docs/reference/config/networking/) into proxy specific configuration.\\n85: \\n86:     - [security](security/). This directory contains [security](https://istio.io/latest/docs/concepts/security/) related code.\\n87: \\n88: - [istio/proxy](https://github.com/istio/proxy). The Istio proxy contains\\n89: extensions to the [Envoy proxy](https://github.com/envoyproxy/envoy) (in the form of\\n90: Envoy filters) that support authentication, authorization, and telemetry collection.\\n91: \\n92: - [istio/ztunnel](https://github.com/istio/ztunnel). The repository contains the Rust implementation of the ztunnel\\n93: component of Ambient mesh.\\n94: \\n95: - [istio/client-go](https://github.com/istio/client-go). This repository defines\\n96:   auto-generated Kubernetes clients for interacting with Istio resources programmatically.\\n97: \\n98: > [!NOTE]\\n99: > Only the `istio/api` and `istio/client-go` repositories expose stable interfaces intended for direct usage as libraries.\\n100: \\n101: ## Issue management\\n102: \\n103: We use GitHub to track all of our bugs and feature requests. Each issue we track has a variety of metadata:\\n104: \\n105: - **Epic**. An epic represents a feature area for Istio as a whole. Epics are fairly broad in scope and are basically product-level things.\\n106: Each issue is ultimately part of an epic.\\n107: \\n108: - **Milestone**. Each issue is assigned a milestone. This is 0.1, 0.2, ..., or 'Nebulous Future'. The milestone indicates when we\\n109: think the issue should get addressed.\\n110: \\n111: - **Priority**. Each issue has a priority which is represented by the column in the [Prioritization](https://github.com/orgs/istio/projects/6) project. Priority can be one of\\n112: P0, P1, P2, or >P2. The priority indicates how important it is to address the issue within the milestone. P0 says that the\\n113: milestone cannot be considered achieved if the issue isn't resolved.\\n114: \\n115: ---\\n116: \\n117: <div align=\\\"center\\\">\\n118:     <picture>\\n119:       <source media=\\\"(prefers-color-scheme: dark)\\\" srcset=\\\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/cncf/artwork/refs/heads/main/other/cncf/horizontal/color-whitetext/cncf-color-whitetext.svg\\\">\\n120:       <source media=\\\"(prefers-color-scheme: light)\\\" srcset=\\\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/cncf/artwork/master/other/cncf/horizontal/color/cncf-color.svg\\\">\\n121:       <img width=\\\"300\\\" alt=\\\"Cloud Native Computing Foundation logo\\\" src=\\\"https://raw.githubusercontent.com/cncf/artwork/refs/heads/main/other/cncf/horizontal/color-whitetext/cncf-color-whitetext.svg\\\">\\n122:     </picture>\\n123:     <p>Istio is a <a href=\\\"https://cncf.io\\\">Cloud Native Computing Foundation</a> project.</p>\\n124: </div>\\n125: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/istio--f8af3cae@3a22a44f97d470ed898e4dfb3b17913cb295c7bf/-/blob/README.md\"}",

{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # Architecture of Istiod\\n2: \\n3: This document describes the high level architecture of the Istio control plane, Istiod.\\n4: Istiod is structured as a modular monolith, housing a wide range of functionality from certificate signing, proxy configuration (XDS), traditional Kubernetes controllers, and more.\\n5: \\n6: ## Proxy Configuration\\n7: \\n8: Istiod's primary role - and most code - is to dynamically configure proxies (Envoy sidecars and ingress, gRPC, ztunnel, and more). This roughly consists of 3 parts:\\n9: 1. Config ingestion (inputs to the system)\\n10: 1. Config translation\\n11: 1. Config serving (XDS)\\n12: \\n13: ### Config Ingestion\\n14: \\n15: Istio reads from over 20 different resources types, and aggregates them together to build the proxy configuration. These resources can be sourced from Kubernetes (via watches), files, or over xDS; Kubernetes is by far the most common usage, though.\\n16: \\n17: Primarily for historical reasons, ingestion is split into a few components.\\n18: \\n19: #### ConfigStore\\n20: \\n21: The `ConfigStore` reads a variety of resources and exposes them over a standard interface (Get, List, etc). These types are wrapped in a common `config.Config` struct, contrasting with typical Kubernetes clients which use per-resource types. The most common is reading from Kubernetes via the `crdclient` package.\\n22: \\n23: ```mermaid\\n24: graph TD\\n25:     subgraph ConfigStore\\n26:         xcs(XDS Client)\\n27:         ccs(CRD Client)\\n28:         fcs(Filesystem Client)\\n29:         acs(Aggregate)\\n30:         xcs-->acs\\n31:         ccs-->acs\\n32:         fcs-->acs\\n33:     end\\n34: ```\\n35: \\n36: #### ServiceDiscovery\\n37: \\n38: The other primary interface is the ServiceDiscovery. Similar to ConfigStore, this aggregates over a variety of resources. However, it does not provide generic resource access, and instead precomputes a variety of service-oriented internal resources, such as `model.Service` and `model.ServiceInstance`.\\n39: \\n40: This is composed of two controllers - one driven from core Kubernetes types (\\\"Kube Controller\\\") and one by Istio types (\\\"ServiceEntry controller\\\").\\n41: \\n42: ```mermaid\\n43: graph TD\\n44:     subgraph Kube Controller\\n45:         s(Services)\\n46:         e(Endpoints)\\n47:         p(Pods)\\n48:         ksi(ServiceInstances)\\n49:         kwi(WorkloadInstances)\\n50:         s-->ksi\\n51:         e-->ksi\\n52:         p-->kwi\\n53:     end\\n54:     subgraph ServiceEntry Controller\\n55:         se(ServiceEntry)\\n56:         we(WorkloadEntry)\\n57:         ssi(ServiceInstances)\\n58:         swi(WorkloadInstances)\\n59:         se-->ssi\\n60:         swi-->ssi\\n61:         we-->swi\\n62:     end\\n63:     kwi-->ssi\\n64:     swi-->ksi\\n65: ```\\n66: \\n67: For the most part this is fairly straight forward. However, we support `ServiceEntry` selecting `Pod`, and `Service` selecting `WorkloadEntry`, which leads to cross-controller communication.\\n68: \\n69: Note: the asymmetry with `Pods` not contributing to Kube controller's `ServiceInstances` is due to the use of `Endpoints` which itself is derived from `Pod` from Kubernetes core.\\n70: \\n71: #### PushContext\\n72: \\n73: `PushContext` is an immutable snapshot of the current state of the world. It is regenerated (usually partially) on each configuration push (more on this below). Due to being a snapshot, most lookups are lock-free.\\n74: \\n75: `PushContext` is built up by querying the above layers. For some simple use cases, this is as simple as storing something like `configstore.List(SomeType)`; in this case, the only difference from directly exposing the configstore is to snapshot the current state. In other cases, some pre-computations and indexes are computed to make later accesses efficient.\\n76: \\n77: #### Endpoints\\n78: \\n79: Endpoints have an optimized code path, as they are by far the most frequently updated resource - in a steady cluster, this will often be the *only* change, caused by scale up/down.\\n80: \\n81: As a result, they do not go through `PushContext`, and changes do not trigger a `PushContext` recomputation. Instead, the current state is incrementally computed based on events from `ServiceDiscovery`.\\n82: \\n83: #### Conclusion\\n84: \\n85: Overall, the high level config ingestion flow:\\n86: \\n87: ```mermaid\\n88: graph TD\\n89:     sd(Service Discovery)\\n90:     cs(ConfigStore)\\n91:     ep(Endpoints)\\n92:     pc(PushContext)\\n93:     sd-->pc\\n94:     cs-->pc\\n95:     sd-->ep\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: ### Config Translation\\n99: \\n100: Config Translation turns the above inputs into the actual types consumed by the connected XDS clients (typically Envoy). This is done by `Generators`, which register a function to build a given type. For example, there is a `RouteGenerator` responsible for building `Routes`. Along with the core Envoy XDS types, there are a few custom Istio types, such as our `NameTable` type used for DNS, as well as debug interfaces.\\n101: \\n102: `Generators` get as input the `Proxy` (a representation of the current client), the current `PushContext` snapshot, and a list of config updates that caused the change.\\n103: \\n104: The `Proxy` as an input parameter is important, and a major distinction from some other XDS implementations. We are not able to statically translate inputs to XDS without per-client information. For example, we rely on the client's labels to determine the set of policies applied. While this is necessary to implement Istio's APIs, it does limit performance substantially.\\n105: \\n106: #### Caching\\n107: \\n108: Config translation typically takes the overwhelming majority of Istiod's resource usage. In particular, protobuf encoding. As a result, caching has been introduced, storing the already encoded `protobuf.Any` for a given resource.\\n109: \\n110: This caching depends on declaring all inputs to the given generator as part of the cache key. This is extremely error-prone, as there is nothing preventing generators from consuming inputs that are *not* part of the key. When this happens, different clients will non-deterministically get incorrect configuration. This type of bug has historically resulted in CVEs.\\n111: \\n112: There are a few ways to prevent these:\\n113: * Only pass in to the generation logic the cache key itself, so no other unaccounted inputs can be used. Unfortunately, this has not been done for any generators today.\\n114: * Be very, very careful.\\n115: * The cache has a builtin test, enabled with `UNSAFE_PILOT_ENABLE_RUNTIME_ASSERTIONS=true`, that runs in CI. This will panic if any key is written to with a different value.\\n116: \\n117: #### Partial Computations\\n118: \\n119: Along with caching, partial computations are a critical performance optimization to ensure that we do not need to build (or send) every resource to every proxy on every change. This is discussed more in the Config Serving section.\\n120: \\n121: ### Config Serving\\n122: \\n123: Config serving is the layer that actually accepts proxy clients, connected over bidirectional gRPC streams, and serve them the required configuration.\\n124: \\n125: We will have two triggers for sending config - requests and pushes.\\n126: \\n127: #### Requests\\n128: \\n129: Requests come from the client specifically asking for a set of resources. This could be requesting the initial set of resources on a new connection, or from a new dependency. For example, a push of `Cluster X` referencing `Endpoint Y` may lead to a request for `Endpoint Y` if it is not already known to the client.\\n130: \\n131: Note that clients can actually send three types of messages - requests, ACKs of previous pushes, and NACKs of previous pushes. Unfortunately, these are not clearly distinguished in the API, so there is some logic to split these out (`shouldRespond`).\\n132: \\n133: #### Pushes\\n134: \\n135: A push occurs when Istiod detects an update of some set of configuration is needed. This results in roughly the same result as a Request (new configuration is pushed to the client), and is just triggered by a different source.\\n136: \\n137: Various components described in Config Ingestion can trigger a Config Update. These are batched up (\\\"debounced\\\"), to avoid excessive activity when many changes happen in succession, and eventually enqueued in the Push Queue.\\n138: \\n139: The Push Queue is mostly a normal queue, but it has some special logic to merge push requests for each given proxy. This results in each proxy having 0 or 1 outstanding push requests; if additional updates come in the existing push request is just expanded.\\n140: \\n141: Another job polls this queue and triggers each client to start a push.\\n142: \\n143: ```mermaid\\n144: graph TD\\n145:     subgraph Config Flow\\n146:         cu(Config Update)\\n147:         db(Debounce)\\n148:         pc(Recompute Push Context)\\n149:         pq(Push Queue)\\n150:         cu-->db\\n151:         db--Trigger Once Steady-->pc\\n152:         pc--Enqueue All Clients-->pq\\n153:     end\\n154:     subgraph Proxy\\n155:         c(Client)\\n156:     end\\n157:     subgraph Pusher\\n158:         pj(Push Job)\\n159:         pj--read-->pq\\n160:         pj--trigger-->c\\n161:     end\\n162: ```\\n163: \\n164: At a high level, each client job will find the correct generator for the request, generate the required configuration, and send it.\\n165: \\n166: #### Optimizations\\n167: \\n168: A naive implementation would simply regenerate all resources, of all subscribed types, for each client, on any configuration change. However, this scales poorly. As a result, we have many levels of optimizations to avoid doing this work.\\n169: \\n170: First, we have a concept of a `Full` push. Only `Full` pushes will recompute `PushContext` on change; otherwise this is skipped and the last `PushContext` is reused. Note: even when `Full`, we try to copy as much from the previous `PushContext` as possible. For example, if only a `WasmPlugin` changed, we would not recompute services indexes.\\n171: Note: `Full` only refers to whether a `PushContext` recomputation is needed. Even within a `Full` push, we keep track of which configuration updates triggered this, so we could have \\\"Full update of Config X\\\" or \\\"Full update of all configs\\\".\\n172: \\n173: Next, for an individual proxy we will check if it could possibly be impacted by the change. For example, we know a sidecar never is impacted by a `Gateway` update, and we can also look at scoping (from `Sidecar.egress.hosts`) to further restrict update scopes.\\n174: \\n175: Once we determine the proxy may be impacted, we determine which *types* may be impacted. For example, we know a `WasmPlugin` does not impact the `Cluster` type, so we can skip generating `Cluster` in this case. Warning: Envoy currently has a bug that *requires* `Endpoints` to be pushed any time the corresponding `Cluster` is pushed, so this optimization is intentionally turned off in this specific case.\\n176: \\n177: Finally, we determine which subset of the type we need to generate. XDS has two modes - \\\"State of the World (SotW)\\\" and \\\"Delta\\\". In SotW, we generally need to generate all resources of the type, even if only one changed. Note that we actually need to *generate* all of them, typically, as we do not store previously generated resources (mostly because they are generated per-client). This also means that whenever we are determining if a change is required, we are doing this based on careful code analysis, not at runtime.\\n178: Despite this expectation in SotW, due to a quirk in the protocol we can actually enable one of our most important optimizations. XDS types form a tree, with CDS and LDS the root of the tree for Envoy. For root types, we *must* always generate the full set of resources - missing resources are treated as deletions.\\n179: However, all other types *cannot* be deleted explicitly, and instead are cleaned up when all references are removed. This means we can send partial updates for non-root types, without deleting unsent resources. This effectively allows doing delta updates over SotW. This optimization is critical for our endpoints generator, ensuring that when a pod scales we only need to update the endpoints within that pod.\\n180: \\n181: Istio currently supports both SotW and Delta protocol. However, the delta implementation is not yet optimized well, so it performs mostly the same as SotW.\\n182: \\n183: ## Controllers\\n184: \\n185: Istiod consists of a collection of controllers. Per Kubernetes, \\\"controllers are control loops that watch the state of your cluster, then make or request changes where needed.\\\"\\n186: \\n187: In Istio, we use the term a bit more liberally. Istio controllers watch more than just the state of *a* cluster -- many are reading from multiple clusters, or even external sources (files and XDS). Generally, Kubernetes controllers are then writing state back to the cluster; Istio does have a few of these controllers, but most of them are centered around driving the [Proxy Configuration](#proxy-configuration).\\n188: \\n189: ### Writing controllers\\n190: \\n191: Istio provides a few helper libraries to get started writing a controller. While these libraries help, there are still a lot of subtleties in correctly writing (and testing!) a controller properly.\\n192: \\n193: To get started writing a controller, review the [Example Controller](../../pkg/kube/controllers/example_test.go).\\n194: \\n195: ### Controllers overview\\n196: \\n197: Below provides a high level overview of controllers in Istiod. For more information about each controller, consulting the controllers Go docs is recommended.\\n198: \\n199: ```mermaid\\n200: graph BT\\n201:     crd(\\\"CRD Watcher\\\")\\n202:     subgraph Service Discovery\\n203:         ksd(\\\"Kubernetes Controller\\\")\\n204:         sesd(\\\"Service Entry Controller\\\")\\n205:         msd(\\\"Memory Controller\\\")\\n206:         asd(\\\"Aggregate\\\")\\n207:         ksd--Join-->asd\\n208:         sesd--Join-->asd\\n209:         msd--Join-->asd\\n210:         ksd<--\\\"Data Sharing\\\"-->sesd\\n211:     end\\n212:     subgraph ConfigStore\\n213:         ccs(\\\"CRD Client\\\")\\n214:         xcs(\\\"XDS Store\\\")\\n215:         fcs(\\\"File Store\\\")\\n216:         mcs(\\\"Memory Store\\\")\\n217:         acs(\\\"Aggregate\\\")\\n218:         ccs--Join-->acs\\n219:         xcs--Join-->acs\\n220:         fcs--Join-->acs\\n221:         mcs--Join-->acs\\n222:     end\\n223:     subgraph VMs\\n224:         vmhc(\\\"Health Check\\\")\\n225:         vmar(\\\"Auto Registration\\\")\\n226:     end\\n227:     subgraph Gateway\\n228:         twc(\\\"Tag Watcher\\\")\\n229:         gdc(\\\"Gateway Deployment\\\")\\n230:         gcc(\\\"Gateway Class\\\")\\n231:         twc--Depends-->gdc\\n232:         gdc-.-gcc\\n233:     end\\n234:     subgraph Ingress\\n235:         ic(\\\"Ingress Controller\\\")\\n236:         isc(\\\"Ingress Status Controller\\\")\\n237:         ic-.-isc\\n238:     end\\n239:     mcsc(\\\"Multicluster Secret\\\")\\n240:     scr(\\\"Credentials Controller\\\")\\n241:     mcsc--\\\"1 per cluster\\\"-->scr\\n242:     mcsc--\\\"1 per cluster\\\"-->ksd\\n243:     crd--Depends-->ccs\\n244: \\n245:     iwhc(\\\"Injection Webhook\\\")\\n246:     vwhc(\\\"Validation Webhook\\\")\\n247:     nsc(\\\"Namespace Controller\\\")\\n248:     ksd--\\\"External Istiod\\\"-->nsc\\n249:     ksd--\\\"External Istiod\\\"-->iwhc\\n250: \\n251:     df(\\\"Discovery Filter\\\")\\n252: \\n253:     axc(\\\"Auto Export Controller\\\")\\n254: \\n255:     mcfg(\\\"Mesh Config\\\")\\n256:     dfc(\\\"Default Revision Controller\\\")\\n257: ```\\n258: \\n259: As you can see, the landscape of controllers is pretty extensive at this point.\\n260: \\n261: [Service Discovery](#ServiceDiscovery) and [Config Store](#ConfigStore) were already discussed above, so do not need more explanation here.\\n262: \\n263: #### Mesh Config\\n264: \\n265: Mesh Config controller is a pretty simple controller, reading from `ConfigMap`(s) (multiple if `SHARED_MESH_CONFIG` is used), processing and merging these into a the typed `MeshConfig`. It then exposes this over a simple `mesh.Watcher`, which just exposes a way to access the current `MeshConfig` and get notified when it changes.\\n266: \\n267: #### Ingress\\n268: \\n269: In addition to `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, Istio supports the `Ingress` core resource type. Like CRDs, the `Ingress` controller implements `ConfigStore`, but a bit differently. `Ingress` resources are converted on the fly to `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, so while the controller reads `Ingress` resources (and a few related types like `IngressClass`), it emits other types. This allows the rest of the code to be unaware of Ingress and just focus on the core types\\n270: \\n271: In addition to this conversion, `Ingress` requires writing the address it can be reached at in status. This is done by the Ingress Status controller.\\n272: \\n273: #### Gateway\\n274: \\n275: Gateway (referring to the [Kubernetes API](http://gateway-api.org/), not the same-named Istio type) works very similarly to [Ingress](#ingress). The Gateway controller also converts Gateway API types into `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, implementing the `ConfigStore` interface.\\n276: \\n277: However, there is also a bit of additional logic. Gateway types have extensive status reporting. Unlike Ingress, this is status reporting is done inline in the main controller, allowing status generation to be done directly in the logic processing the resources.\\n278: \\n279: Additionally, Gateway involves two components writing to the cluster:\\n280: * The Gateway Class controller is a simple controller that just writes a default `GatewayClass` object describing our implementation.\\n281: * The Gateway Deployment controller enables users to create a Gateway which actually provisions the underlying resources for the implementation (Deployment and Service). This is more like a traditional \\\"operator\\\". Part of this logic is determining which Istiod revision should handle the resource based on `istio.io/rev` labeling (mirroring sidecar injection); as a result, this takes a dependency on the \\\"Tag Watcher\\\" controller.\\n282: \\n283: #### CRD Watcher\\n284: \\n285: For watches against custom types (CRDs), we want to gracefully handle missing CRDs. Naively starting informers against the missing types would result in errors and blocking startup. Instead, we introduce a \\\"CRD Watcher\\\" component that watches the CRDs in the cluster to determine if they are available or not.\\n286: \\n287: This is consumed in two ways:\\n288: * Some components just block on `watcher.WaitForCRD(...)` before doing the work they need.\\n289: * `kclient.NewDelayedInformer` can also fully abstract this away, by providing a client that handles this behind the scenes.\\n290: \\n291: #### Credentials Controller\\n292: \\n293: The Credentials controller exposes access to TLS certificate information, stored in cluster as `Secrets`. Aside from simply accessing certificates, it also has an authorization component that can verify whether a requester has access to read `Secret`s in its namespace.\\n294: \\n295: #### Discovery Filter\\n296: \\n297: The Discovery Filter controller is used to implement the `discoverySelectors` field of `MeshConfig`. This controller reads `Namespace`s in the cluster to determine if they should be \\\"selected\\\". Many controllers consumer this filter to only process a subset of configurations.\\n298: \\n299: #### Multicluster\\n300: \\n301: Various controllers read from multiple clusters.\\n302: \\n303: This is rooted in the Multicluster Secret controller, which reads `kubeconfig` files (stored as `Secrets`), and creating Kubernetes clients for each. The controller allows registering handlers which can process Add/Update/Delete of clusters.\\n304: \\n305: This has two implementations:\\n306: * The Credentials controller is responsible for reading TLS certificates, stored as Secrets.\\n307: * The Kubernetes Service Discovery controller is a bit of a monolith, and spins off a bunch of other sub-controllers in addition to the core service discovery controller.\\n308: \\n309: Because of the monolithic complexity it helps to see this magnified a bit:\\n310: \\n311: ```mermaid\\n312: graph BT\\n313:     mcsc(\\\"Multicluster Secret\\\")\\n314:     scr(\\\"Credentials Controller\\\")\\n315:     ksd(\\\"Kubernetes Service Controller\\\")\\n316:     nsc(\\\"Namespace Controller\\\")\\n317:     wes(\\\"Workload Entry Store\\\")\\n318:     iwh(\\\"Injection Patcher\\\")\\n319:     aex(\\\"Auto Service Export\\\")\\n320:     scr-->mcsc\\n321:     ksd-->mcsc\\n322:     nsc-->ksd\\n323:     wes-->ksd\\n324:     iwh-->ksd\\n325:     aex-->ksd\\n326: ```\\n327: \\n328: #### VMs\\n329: \\n330: Virtual Machine support consists of two controllers.\\n331: \\n332: The Auto Registration controller is pretty unique as a controller - the inputs to the controller are XDS connections. In response to each XDS connection, a `WorkloadEntry` is created to register the XDS client (which is generally `istio-proxy` running on a VM) to the mesh. This `WorkloadEntry` is tied to the lifecycle of the connection, with some logic to ensure that temporary downtime (reconnecting, etc) does not remove the `WorkloadEntry`.\\n333: \\n334: The Health Check controller additionally controls the health status of the `WorkloadEntry`. The health is reported over the XDS client and synced with the `WorkloadEntry`.\\n335: \\n336: #### Webhooks\\n337: \\n338: Istio contains both Validation and Mutating webhook configurations. These need a `caBundle` specified in order to provision the TLS trust. Because Istiod's CA certificate is somewhat dynamic, this is patched at runtime (rather than part of the install). The webhook controllers handle this patching.\\n339: \\n340: These controllers are very similar but are distinct components for a variety of reasons.\\n341: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/istio--f8af3cae@3a22a44f97d470ed898e4dfb3b17913cb295c7bf/-/blob/architecture/networking/pilot.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # Architecture of Istiod\n2: \n3: This document describes the high level architecture of the Istio control plane, Istiod.\n4: Istiod is structured as a modular monolith, housing a wide range of functionality from certificate signing, proxy configuration (XDS), traditional Kubernetes controllers, and more.\n5: \n6: ## Proxy Configuration\n7: \n8: Istiod's primary role - and most code - is to dynamically configure proxies (Envoy sidecars and ingress, gRPC, ztunnel, and more). This roughly consists of 3 parts:\n9: 1. Config ingestion (inputs to the system)\n10: 1. Config translation\n11: 1. Config serving (XDS)\n12: \n13: ### Config Ingestion\n14: \n15: Istio reads from over 20 different resources types, and aggregates them together to build the proxy configuration. These resources can be sourced from Kubernetes (via watches), files, or over xDS; Kubernetes is by far the most common usage, though.\n16: \n17: Primarily for historical reasons, ingestion is split into a few components.\n18: \n19: #### ConfigStore\n20: \n21: The `ConfigStore` reads a variety of resources and exposes them over a standard interface (Get, List, etc). These types are wrapped in a common `config.Config` struct, contrasting with typical Kubernetes clients which use per-resource types. The most common is reading from Kubernetes via the `crdclient` package.\n22: \n23: ```mermaid\n24: graph TD\n25:     subgraph ConfigStore\n26:         xcs(XDS Client)\n27:         ccs(CRD Client)\n28:         fcs(Filesystem Client)\n29:         acs(Aggregate)\n30:         xcs-->acs\n31:         ccs-->acs\n32:         fcs-->acs\n33:     end\n34: ```\n35: \n36: #### ServiceDiscovery\n37: \n38: The other primary interface is the ServiceDiscovery. Similar to ConfigStore, this aggregates over a variety of resources. However, it does not provide generic resource access, and instead precomputes a variety of service-oriented internal resources, such as `model.Service` and `model.ServiceInstance`.\n39: \n40: This is composed of two controllers - one driven from core Kubernetes types (\"Kube Controller\") and one by Istio types (\"ServiceEntry controller\").\n41: \n42: ```mermaid\n43: graph TD\n44:     subgraph Kube Controller\n45:         s(Services)\n46:         e(Endpoints)\n47:         p(Pods)\n48:         ksi(ServiceInstances)\n49:         kwi(WorkloadInstances)\n50:         s-->ksi\n51:         e-->ksi\n52:         p-->kwi\n53:     end\n54:     subgraph ServiceEntry Controller\n55:         se(ServiceEntry)\n56:         we(WorkloadEntry)\n57:         ssi(ServiceInstances)\n58:         swi(WorkloadInstances)\n59:         se-->ssi\n60:         swi-->ssi\n61:         we-->swi\n62:     end\n63:     kwi-->ssi\n64:     swi-->ksi\n65: ```\n66: \n67: For the most part this is fairly straight forward. However, we support `ServiceEntry` selecting `Pod`, and `Service` selecting `WorkloadEntry`, which leads to cross-controller communication.\n68: \n69: Note: the asymmetry with `Pods` not contributing to Kube controller's `ServiceInstances` is due to the use of `Endpoints` which itself is derived from `Pod` from Kubernetes core.\n70: \n71: #### PushContext\n72: \n73: `PushContext` is an immutable snapshot of the current state of the world. It is regenerated (usually partially) on each configuration push (more on this below). Due to being a snapshot, most lookups are lock-free.\n74: \n75: `PushContext` is built up by querying the above layers. For some simple use cases, this is as simple as storing something like `configstore.List(SomeType)`; in this case, the only difference from directly exposing the configstore is to snapshot the current state. In other cases, some pre-computations and indexes are computed to make later accesses efficient.\n76: \n77: #### Endpoints\n78: \n79: Endpoints have an optimized code path, as they are by far the most frequently updated resource - in a steady cluster, this will often be the *only* change, caused by scale up/down.\n80: \n81: As a result, they do not go through `PushContext`, and changes do not trigger a `PushContext` recomputation. Instead, the current state is incrementally computed based on events from `ServiceDiscovery`.\n82: \n83: #### Conclusion\n84: \n85: Overall, the high level config ingestion flow:\n86: \n87: ```mermaid\n88: graph TD\n89:     sd(Service Discovery)\n90:     cs(ConfigStore)\n91:     ep(Endpoints)\n92:     pc(PushContext)\n93:     sd-->pc\n94:     cs-->pc\n95:     sd-->ep\n96: ```\n97: \n98: ### Config Translation\n99: \n100: Config Translation turns the above inputs into the actual types consumed by the connected XDS clients (typically Envoy). This is done by `Generators`, which register a function to build a given type. For example, there is a `RouteGenerator` responsible for building `Routes`. Along with the core Envoy XDS types, there are a few custom Istio types, such as our `NameTable` type used for DNS, as well as debug interfaces.\n101: \n102: `Generators` get as input the `Proxy` (a representation of the current client), the current `PushContext` snapshot, and a list of config updates that caused the change.\n103: \n104: The `Proxy` as an input parameter is important, and a major distinction from some other XDS implementations. We are not able to statically translate inputs to XDS without per-client information. For example, we rely on the client's labels to determine the set of policies applied. While this is necessary to implement Istio's APIs, it does limit performance substantially.\n105: \n106: #### Caching\n107: \n108: Config translation typically takes the overwhelming majority of Istiod's resource usage. In particular, protobuf encoding. As a result, caching has been introduced, storing the already encoded `protobuf.Any` for a given resource.\n109: \n110: This caching depends on declaring all inputs to the given generator as part of the cache key. This is extremely error-prone, as there is nothing preventing generators from consuming inputs that are *not* part of the key. When this happens, different clients will non-deterministically get incorrect configuration. This type of bug has historically resulted in CVEs.\n111: \n112: There are a few ways to prevent these:\n113: * Only pass in to the generation logic the cache key itself, so no other unaccounted inputs can be used. Unfortunately, this has not been done for any generators today.\n114: * Be very, very careful.\n115: * The cache has a builtin test, enabled with `UNSAFE_PILOT_ENABLE_RUNTIME_ASSERTIONS=true`, that runs in CI. This will panic if any key is written to with a different value.\n116: \n117: #### Partial Computations\n118: \n119: Along with caching, partial computations are a critical performance optimization to ensure that we do not need to build (or send) every resource to every proxy on every change. This is discussed more in the Config Serving section.\n120: \n121: ### Config Serving\n122: \n123: Config serving is the layer that actually accepts proxy clients, connected over bidirectional gRPC streams, and serve them the required configuration.\n124: \n125: We will have two triggers for sending config - requests and pushes.\n126: \n127: #### Requests\n128: \n129: Requests come from the client specifically asking for a set of resources. This could be requesting the initial set of resources on a new connection, or from a new dependency. For example, a push of `Cluster X` referencing `Endpoint Y` may lead to a request for `Endpoint Y` if it is not already known to the client.\n130: \n131: Note that clients can actually send three types of messages - requests, ACKs of previous pushes, and NACKs of previous pushes. Unfortunately, these are not clearly distinguished in the API, so there is some logic to split these out (`shouldRespond`).\n132: \n133: #### Pushes\n134: \n135: A push occurs when Istiod detects an update of some set of configuration is needed. This results in roughly the same result as a Request (new configuration is pushed to the client), and is just triggered by a different source.\n136: \n137: Various components described in Config Ingestion can trigger a Config Update. These are batched up (\"debounced\"), to avoid excessive activity when many changes happen in succession, and eventually enqueued in the Push Queue.\n138: \n139: The Push Queue is mostly a normal queue, but it has some special logic to merge push requests for each given proxy. This results in each proxy having 0 or 1 outstanding push requests; if additional updates come in the existing push request is just expanded.\n140: \n141: Another job polls this queue and triggers each client to start a push.\n142: \n143: ```mermaid\n144: graph TD\n145:     subgraph Config Flow\n146:         cu(Config Update)\n147:         db(Debounce)\n148:         pc(Recompute Push Context)\n149:         pq(Push Queue)\n150:         cu-->db\n151:         db--Trigger Once Steady-->pc\n152:         pc--Enqueue All Clients-->pq\n153:     end\n154:     subgraph Proxy\n155:         c(Client)\n156:     end\n157:     subgraph Pusher\n158:         pj(Push Job)\n159:         pj--read-->pq\n160:         pj--trigger-->c\n161:     end\n162: ```\n163: \n164: At a high level, each client job will find the correct generator for the request, generate the required configuration, and send it.\n165: \n166: #### Optimizations\n167: \n168: A naive implementation would simply regenerate all resources, of all subscribed types, for each client, on any configuration change. However, this scales poorly. As a result, we have many levels of optimizations to avoid doing this work.\n169: \n170: First, we have a concept of a `Full` push. Only `Full` pushes will recompute `PushContext` on change; otherwise this is skipped and the last `PushContext` is reused. Note: even when `Full`, we try to copy as much from the previous `PushContext` as possible. For example, if only a `WasmPlugin` changed, we would not recompute services indexes.\n171: Note: `Full` only refers to whether a `PushContext` recomputation is needed. Even within a `Full` push, we keep track of which configuration updates triggered this, so we could have \"Full update of Config X\" or \"Full update of all configs\".\n172: \n173: Next, for an individual proxy we will check if it could possibly be impacted by the change. For example, we know a sidecar never is impacted by a `Gateway` update, and we can also look at scoping (from `Sidecar.egress.hosts`) to further restrict update scopes.\n174: \n175: Once we determine the proxy may be impacted, we determine which *types* may be impacted. For example, we know a `WasmPlugin` does not impact the `Cluster` type, so we can skip generating `Cluster` in this case. Warning: Envoy currently has a bug that *requires* `Endpoints` to be pushed any time the corresponding `Cluster` is pushed, so this optimization is intentionally turned off in this specific case.\n176: \n177: Finally, we determine which subset of the type we need to generate. XDS has two modes - \"State of the World (SotW)\" and \"Delta\". In SotW, we generally need to generate all resources of the type, even if only one changed. Note that we actually need to *generate* all of them, typically, as we do not store previously generated resources (mostly because they are generated per-client). This also means that whenever we are determining if a change is required, we are doing this based on careful code analysis, not at runtime.\n178: Despite this expectation in SotW, due to a quirk in the protocol we can actually enable one of our most important optimizations. XDS types form a tree, with CDS and LDS the root of the tree for Envoy. For root types, we *must* always generate the full set of resources - missing resources are treated as deletions.\n179: However, all other types *cannot* be deleted explicitly, and instead are cleaned up when all references are removed. This means we can send partial updates for non-root types, without deleting unsent resources. This effectively allows doing delta updates over SotW. This optimization is critical for our endpoints generator, ensuring that when a pod scales we only need to update the endpoints within that pod.\n180: \n181: Istio currently supports both SotW and Delta protocol. However, the delta implementation is not yet optimized well, so it performs mostly the same as SotW.\n182: \n183: ## Controllers\n184: \n185: Istiod consists of a collection of controllers. Per Kubernetes, \"controllers are control loops that watch the state of your cluster, then make or request changes where needed.\"\n186: \n187: In Istio, we use the term a bit more liberally. Istio controllers watch more than just the state of *a* cluster -- many are reading from multiple clusters, or even external sources (files and XDS). Generally, Kubernetes controllers are then writing state back to the cluster; Istio does have a few of these controllers, but most of them are centered around driving the [Proxy Configuration](#proxy-configuration).\n188: \n189: ### Writing controllers\n190: \n191: Istio provides a few helper libraries to get started writing a controller. While these libraries help, there are still a lot of subtleties in correctly writing (and testing!) a controller properly.\n192: \n193: To get started writing a controller, review the [Example Controller](../../pkg/kube/controllers/example_test.go).\n194: \n195: ### Controllers overview\n196: \n197: Below provides a high level overview of controllers in Istiod. For more information about each controller, consulting the controllers Go docs is recommended.\n198: \n199: ```mermaid\n200: graph BT\n201:     crd(\"CRD Watcher\")\n202:     subgraph Service Discovery\n203:         ksd(\"Kubernetes Controller\")\n204:         sesd(\"Service Entry Controller\")\n205:         msd(\"Memory Controller\")\n206:         asd(\"Aggregate\")\n207:         ksd--Join-->asd\n208:         sesd--Join-->asd\n209:         msd--Join-->asd\n210:         ksd<--\"Data Sharing\"-->sesd\n211:     end\n212:     subgraph ConfigStore\n213:         ccs(\"CRD Client\")\n214:         xcs(\"XDS Store\")\n215:         fcs(\"File Store\")\n216:         mcs(\"Memory Store\")\n217:         acs(\"Aggregate\")\n218:         ccs--Join-->acs\n219:         xcs--Join-->acs\n220:         fcs--Join-->acs\n221:         mcs--Join-->acs\n222:     end\n223:     subgraph VMs\n224:         vmhc(\"Health Check\")\n225:         vmar(\"Auto Registration\")\n226:     end\n227:     subgraph Gateway\n228:         twc(\"Tag Watcher\")\n229:         gdc(\"Gateway Deployment\")\n230:         gcc(\"Gateway Class\")\n231:         twc--Depends-->gdc\n232:         gdc-.-gcc\n233:     end\n234:     subgraph Ingress\n235:         ic(\"Ingress Controller\")\n236:         isc(\"Ingress Status Controller\")\n237:         ic-.-isc\n238:     end\n239:     mcsc(\"Multicluster Secret\")\n240:     scr(\"Credentials Controller\")\n241:     mcsc--\"1 per cluster\"-->scr\n242:     mcsc--\"1 per cluster\"-->ksd\n243:     crd--Depends-->ccs\n244: \n245:     iwhc(\"Injection Webhook\")\n246:     vwhc(\"Validation Webhook\")\n247:     nsc(\"Namespace Controller\")\n248:     ksd--\"External Istiod\"-->nsc\n249:     ksd--\"External Istiod\"-->iwhc\n250: \n251:     df(\"Discovery Filter\")\n252: \n253:     axc(\"Auto Export Controller\")\n254: \n255:     mcfg(\"Mesh Config\")\n256:     dfc(\"Default Revision Controller\")\n257: ```\n258: \n259: As you can see, the landscape of controllers is pretty extensive at this point.\n260: \n261: [Service Discovery](#ServiceDiscovery) and [Config Store](#ConfigStore) were already discussed above, so do not need more explanation here.\n262: \n263: #### Mesh Config\n264: \n265: Mesh Config controller is a pretty simple controller, reading from `ConfigMap`(s) (multiple if `SHARED_MESH_CONFIG` is used), processing and merging these into a the typed `MeshConfig`. It then exposes this over a simple `mesh.Watcher`, which just exposes a way to access the current `MeshConfig` and get notified when it changes.\n266: \n267: #### Ingress\n268: \n269: In addition to `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, Istio supports the `Ingress` core resource type. Like CRDs, the `Ingress` controller implements `ConfigStore`, but a bit differently. `Ingress` resources are converted on the fly to `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, so while the controller reads `Ingress` resources (and a few related types like `IngressClass`), it emits other types. This allows the rest of the code to be unaware of Ingress and just focus on the core types\n270: \n271: In addition to this conversion, `Ingress` requires writing the address it can be reached at in status. This is done by the Ingress Status controller.\n272: \n273: #### Gateway\n274: \n275: Gateway (referring to the [Kubernetes API](http://gateway-api.org/), not the same-named Istio type) works very similarly to [Ingress](#ingress). The Gateway controller also converts Gateway API types into `VirtualService` and `Gateway`, implementing the `ConfigStore` interface.\n276: \n277: However, there is also a bit of additional logic. Gateway types have extensive status reporting. Unlike Ingress, this is status reporting is done inline in the main controller, allowing status generation to be done directly in the logic processing the resources.\n278: \n279: Additionally, Gateway involves two components writing to the cluster:\n280: * The Gateway Class controller is a simple controller that just writes a default `GatewayClass` object describing our implementation.\n281: * The Gateway Deployment controller enables users to create a Gateway which actually provisions the underlying resources for the implementation (Deployment and Service). This is more like a traditional \"operator\". Part of this logic is determining which Istiod revision should handle the resource based on `istio.io/rev` labeling (mirroring sidecar injection); as a result, this takes a dependency on the \"Tag Watcher\" controller.\n282: \n283: #### CRD Watcher\n284: \n285: For watches against custom types (CRDs), we want to gracefully handle missing CRDs. Naively starting informers against the missing types would result in errors and blocking startup. Instead, we introduce a \"CRD Watcher\" component that watches the CRDs in the cluster to determine if they are available or not.\n286: \n287: This is consumed in two ways:\n288: * Some components just block on `watcher.WaitForCRD(...)` before doing the work they need.\n289: * `kclient.NewDelayedInformer` can also fully abstract this away, by providing a client that handles this behind the scenes.\n290: \n291: #### Credentials Controller\n292: \n293: The Credentials controller exposes access to TLS certificate information, stored in cluster as `Secrets`. Aside from simply accessing certificates, it also has an authorization component that can verify whether a requester has access to read `Secret`s in its namespace.\n294: \n295: #### Discovery Filter\n296: \n297: The Discovery Filter controller is used to implement the `discoverySelectors` field of `MeshConfig`. This controller reads `Namespace`s in the cluster to determine if they should be \"selected\". Many controllers consumer this filter to only process a subset of configurations.\n298: \n299: #### Multicluster\n300: \n301: Various controllers read from multiple clusters.\n302: \n303: This is rooted in the Multicluster Secret controller, which reads `kubeconfig` files (stored as `Secrets`), and creating Kubernetes clients for each. The controller allows registering handlers which can process Add/Update/Delete of clusters.\n304: \n305: This has two implementations:\n306: * The Credentials controller is responsible for reading TLS certificates, stored as Secrets.\n307: * The Kubernetes Service Discovery controller is a bit of a monolith, and spins off a bunch of other sub-controllers in addition to the core service discovery controller.\n308: \n309: Because of the monolithic complexity it helps to see this magnified a bit:\n310: \n311: ```mermaid\n312: graph BT\n313:     mcsc(\"Multicluster Secret\")\n314:     scr(\"Credentials Controller\")\n315:     ksd(\"Kubernetes Service Controller\")\n316:     nsc(\"Namespace Controller\")\n317:     wes(\"Workload Entry Store\")\n318:     iwh(\"Injection Patcher\")\n319:     aex(\"Auto Service Export\")\n320:     scr-->mcsc\n321:     ksd-->mcsc\n322:     nsc-->ksd\n323:     wes-->ksd\n324:     iwh-->ksd\n325:     aex-->ksd\n326: ```\n327: \n328: #### VMs\n329: \n330: Virtual Machine support consists of two controllers.\n331: \n332: The Auto Registration controller is pretty unique as a controller - the inputs to the controller are XDS connections. In response to each XDS connection, a `WorkloadEntry` is created to register the XDS client (which is generally `istio-proxy` running on a VM) to the mesh. This `WorkloadEntry` is tied to the lifecycle of the connection, with some logic to ensure that temporary downtime (reconnecting, etc) does not remove the `WorkloadEntry`.\n333: \n334: The Health Check controller additionally controls the health status of the `WorkloadEntry`. The health is reported over the XDS client and synced with the `WorkloadEntry`.\n335: \n336: #### Webhooks\n337: \n338: Istio contains both Validation and Mutating webhook configurations. These need a `caBundle` specified in order to provision the TLS trust. Because Istiod's CA certificate is somewhat dynamic, this is patched at runtime (rather than part of the install). The webhook controllers handle this patching.\n339: \n340: These controllers are very similar but are distinct components for a variety of reasons.\n341: ",

{
  "content": "# Istio Control Plane Developer Onboarding Guide\n\nWelcome to the Istio control plane development community! This guide will help you set up your development environment, understand the architecture, and make your first contribution to Istiod, Istio's core control plane component.\n\n## Table of Contents\n\n1. [Build Prerequisites](#build-prerequisites)\n2. [Architecture Overview](#architecture-overview)\n3. [First Contribution Workflow](#first-contribution-workflow)\n\n---\n\n## Build Prerequisites\n\n### Required Go Version\n\nIstio requires **Go 1.25.0 or later**. Verify your installation:\n\n```bash\ngo version\n```\n\n### Environment Setup\n\nIstio uses a modular build system that can operate in two modes:\n\n#### Option A: Native Build (Recommended for Development)\n\nIf you have Go toolchain installed locally, you can build directly:\n\n```bash\n# No additional setup needed - the Makefile will configure itself\nmake build\n```\n\n#### Option B: Container-Based Build\n\nTo use Docker for a reproducible build environment:\n\n```bash\nexport BUILD_WITH_CONTAINER=1\nmake build\n```\n\n**Note:** The build system will automatically detect your environment and set up necessary variables via `common/scripts/setup_env.sh`.\n\n### Cloning the Repository with Submodules\n\nClone Istio with all submodules:\n\n```bash\ngit clone --recurse-submodules https://github.com/istio/istio.git\ncd istio\n```\n\nIf you've already cloned without submodules, initialize them:\n\n```bash\ngit submodule update --init --recursive\n```\n\n### Key Build Targets\n\nThe Istio build system provides several targets via `Makefile.core.mk`:\n\n#### Building Core Control Plane Binaries\n\n**Build all binaries (pilot-discovery, istioctl, pilot-agent):**\n\n```bash\nmake build\n```\n\nThis generates binaries in `$GOBIN` (typically `$GOPATH/bin`).\n\n**Build Linux-specific binaries (for container images):**\n\n```bash\nmake build-linux\n```\n\n**Build individual components:**\n\n```bash\n# pilot-discovery - the main XDS server and control plane\nmake pilot-discovery\n\n# istioctl - the command-line administration tool\nmake istioctl\n\n# pilot-agent - the data plane proxy agent\nmake pilot-agent\n```\n\n**Key binaries defined in Makefile.core.mk (lines 209-223):**\n\n- `./pilot/cmd/pilot-discovery` - Main control plane server\n- `./istioctl/cmd/istioctl` - CLI tool\n- `./pilot/cmd/pilot-agent` - Agent running on data plane\n- `./security/tools/generate_cert` - Certificate generation utility\n\n#### Understanding Build Dependencies\n\nThe build process depends on:\n\n1. **Envoy**: Downloaded by `make init` from pre-built artifacts\n2. **Go modules**: Managed through `go.mod` and `go.sum`\n3. **Protocol buffers**: Code generation handled by `tools/proto/proto.mk`\n\nTo initialize these dependencies:\n\n```bash\nmake init\n```\n\n### Running Tests\n\n#### Unit Tests\n\nRun all unit tests with race condition detection:\n\n```bash\nmake test\n```\n\nThis internally runs `make racetest` and outputs JUnit-formatted results to `$ARTIFACTS/junit.xml`.\n\n**Run tests for a specific package:**\n\n```bash\ngo test -race ./pilot/pkg/xds/...\ngo test -race ./pkg/kube/inject/...\ngo test -race ./security/pkg/...\n```\n\n**Run tests with verbose output:**\n\n```bash\ngo test -v -race ./pilot/pkg/...\n```\n\n**Run fuzz tests:**\n\n```bash\ngo test -tags=integ ./tests/integration/pilot/...\n```\n\n#### Integration Tests\n\nIstio's integration tests require a Kubernetes cluster. They are tagged with the `integ` build tag:\n\n```bash\n# From the repository root, run integration tests\ngo test -tags=integ ./tests/integration/pilot/...\n```\n\nIntegration tests are located in `tests/integration/` and organized by component:\n\n- `tests/integration/pilot/` - Traffic management tests\n- `tests/integration/security/` - Security and mTLS tests\n- `tests/integration/ambient/` - Ambient mesh tests\n- `tests/integration/telemetry/` - Observability tests\n\n### Verifying Your Build\n\nAfter building, verify the binaries work:\n\n```bash\n# Check pilot-discovery help\n$GOPATH/bin/pilot-discovery --help\n\n# Check version\n$GOPATH/bin/pilot-discovery version\n```\n\n---\n\n## Architecture Overview\n\n### Control Plane Components and Responsibilities\n\nIstiod is Istio's unified control plane, implementing what were historically three separate components:\n\n- **Pilot**: Manages traffic configuration (routing, load balancing, circuit breakers)\n- **Citadel**: Provides certificate management and mTLS security\n- **Galley**: Handles configuration distribution\n\nAll three are now merged into the single **istiod** binary (`pilot/cmd/pilot-discovery`).\n\n#### Component Organization\n\n```\npilot/                          - Traffic management & service discovery\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/xds/                    - XDS protocol implementation\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/networking/             - Envoy config generation (routes, clusters, listeners)\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/model/                  - Core data models (Proxy, PushContext, Service)\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/config/                 - Kubernetes configuration ingestion\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/serviceregistry/        - Service discovery from K8s/ServiceEntry\n\u2514\u2500\u2500 cmd/pilot-discovery/        - Main entry point\n\nsecurity/                       - mTLS and certificate management\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/ca/                     - Certificate authority implementation\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 pkg/authn/                  - Authentication policy enforcement\n\u2514\u2500\u2500 pkg/authz/                  - Authorization policy implementation\n\npkg/                            - Shared utilities\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 kube/                       - Kubernetes client integration\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 xds/                        - XDS server infrastructure\n\u251c\u2500\u2500 model/                      - Shared data models\n\u2514\u2500\u2500 ...\n```\n\n### The xDS Protocol: Service Configuration Delivery\n\nThe **xDS (Extensible Discovery Service)** protocol is the core mechanism through which Istiod pushes configuration to Envoy proxies (and other clients like gRPC, ztunnel).\n\n#### xDS Resource Types\n\nEnvoy clients request configuration through xDS:\n\n- **Listeners (LDS)**: Network listeners for incoming/outgoing traffic\n- **Routes (RDS)**: HTTP routing rules\n- **Clusters (CDS)**: Backend services and load balancing policies\n- **Endpoints (EDS)**: Individual service instances and their health status\n\nThese are interdependent: a route references a cluster, a listener references a route configuration.\n\n#### End-to-End Service Discovery to xDS Push\n\nHere's how a service discovery event flows through Istiod to update Envoy:\n\n```\n1. Kubernetes Event Occurs\n   \u2514\u2500> Pod starts/stops, Service scales, or ServiceEntry is created\n\n2. Service Discovery Layer (pilot/pkg/serviceregistry/)\n   \u2514\u2500> Detects changes via K8s informers\n   \u2514\u2500> Updates internal ServiceInstance and Service models\n\n3. Config Ingestion (architecture/networking/pilot.md)\n   \u2514\u2500> ConfigStore aggregates Kubernetes resources (Services, VirtualServices, DestinationRules)\n   \u2514\u2500> PushContext built - immutable snapshot of current configuration state\n\n4. Push Queue\n   \u2514\u2500> Change triggers debounce to avoid excessive updates\n   \u2514\u2500> Enqueued for all affected proxies\n   \u2514\u2500> Per-proxy merge logic ensures efficient batching\n\n5. Config Generation (pilot/pkg/networking/)\n   \u251c\u2500> ClusterBuilder: Generates Envoy Clusters + Endpoints\n   \u251c\u2500> ListenerBuilder: Generates Envoy Listeners\n   \u251c\u2500> RouteBuilder: Generates VirtualHosts + Routes (RDS)\n   \u2514\u2500> Results are cached as protobuf.Any for reuse\n\n6. XDS Server (pilot/pkg/xds/)\n   \u2514\u2500> Sends generated config to Envoy over bidirectional gRPC stream\n   \u2514\u2500> Supports both SotW (State of the World) and Delta xDS protocols\n   \u2514\u2500> Tracks client subscriptions and only sends changed resources\n\n7. Envoy Applies Configuration\n   \u2514\u2500> Updates routing rules, load balancing, circuit breakers\n   \u2514\u2500> Traffic flows according to new configuration\n```\n\n### Key Packages and Their Roles\n\n#### pilot/pkg/xds/ - XDS Protocol Server\n\n**Location**: `pilot/pkg/xds/`\n\nImplements the gRPC server that Envoy connects to:\n\n- `xds_server.go`: Main server accepting client connections\n- `xdsgen.go`: Config generation and versioning logic\n- `delta.go`: Delta xDS protocol implementation\n- `ads.go`: Aggregated Discovery Service (streaming requests)\n\n**Key concepts:**\n\n```go\n// DiscoveryServer - main server accepting xDS requests\ntype DiscoveryServer struct {\n    // Manages clients and subscriptions\n    // Triggers config pushes when changes occur\n}\n\n// Connection - represents a single Envoy proxy connection\ntype Connection struct {\n    proxy *model.Proxy\n    // Tracks subscribed resource types\n    // Handles bidirectional streaming\n}\n```\n\n**Example**: When a VirtualService is updated, the server:\n1. Detects the change\n2. Computes which proxies are affected\n3. Generates new Route configs via RouteGenerator\n4. Streams updates to affected clients\n\n#### pilot/pkg/networking/core/ - Envoy Config Builders\n\n**Location**: `pilot/pkg/networking/core/`\n\nTranslates Istio configuration into Envoy protobuf format:\n\n- `cluster_builder.go`: Builds `Cluster` and `ClusterLoadAssignment` (endpoints)\n- `listener_builder.go`: Builds `Listener` with network/HTTP filters\n- `httproute.go`: Builds `RouteConfiguration` with virtual hosts and routes\n- `networkfilter.go`: Network-level filters (TCP, TLS)\n\n**Example usage in code**:\n\n```go\n// From pilot/pkg/xds/\nfunc (s *DiscoveryServer) generateClusters(proxy *model.Proxy, push *model.PushContext) {\n    cb := &clusterBuilder{proxy: proxy, req: req, push: push}\n    // Generates all Envoy Clusters for this proxy\n    clusters := cb.buildClusters()\n}\n```\n\n#### pilot/pkg/model/ - Core Data Structures\n\n**Location**: `pilot/pkg/model/`\n\nCore types representing Istio's understanding of the service mesh:\n\n- `proxy.go`: `Proxy` - represents an Envoy sidecar/gateway\n- `context.go`: `PushContext` - immutable snapshot of configuration\n- `service.go`: `Service` - internal representation of a Kubernetes Service\n- `config.go`: `Config` - wrapper for all Istio resource types\n\n**PushContext structure** (key to efficiency):\n\n```go\ntype PushContext struct {\n    // Immutable snapshot of mesh state at a point in time\n    // Enables lock-free reads during config generation\n\n    // Indexes for fast lookups\n    services map[string]*Service\n    virtualServices map[string]*VirtualService\n    destinationRules map[string]*DestinationRule\n\n    // Per-proxy computed values\n    sidecarScopes map[string]*SidecarScope\n}\n```\n\n#### pilot/pkg/serviceregistry/ - Service Discovery\n\n**Location**: `pilot/pkg/serviceregistry/`\n\nAggregates service information from multiple sources:\n\n- `kube/`: Kubernetes controller watching Services, Endpoints, Pods\n- `external.go`: ServiceEntry resources\n- `aggregate.go`: Merges all sources\n\n**Example**: When a Pod starts:\n\n```go\n// K8s informer detects Pod event\n// -> Updates Endpoints\n// -> ServiceDiscovery computes ServiceInstance\n// -> Triggers XDS push to affected proxies\n```\n\n#### security/pkg/ - Certificate and Policy Management\n\n**Location**: `security/pkg/`\n\nManages mTLS certificates and authorization:\n\n- `ca/`: Certificate authority implementation\n- `authn/`: Authentication (mutual TLS) policy\n- `authz/`: Authorization (RBAC) policy\n\n**Integration with Istiod**: Security policies are translated into Envoy filters during config generation:\n\n```go\n// In pilot/pkg/networking/\n// Security policies become Envoy filters in listener/route configuration\n```\n\n### Configuration Flow
